<p><b>Bosnia and Herzegovina</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'b' in 'buy'">b</span><span title="/ɒ/: 'o' in 'body'">ɒ</span><span title="'z' in 'zoom'">z</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/iː/ or /ɪ/: 'y' in 'happy'">i</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span> <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span></span> <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="'h' in 'hi'">h</span><span title="/ɛər/: 'are' in 'bare'">ɛər</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ɡ/: 'g' in 'guy'">ɡ</span><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'v' in 'vie'">v</span><span title="/iː/: 'ee' in 'fleece'">iː</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span>, -<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="'h' in 'hi'">h</span><span title="/ɜːr/: 'ur' in 'fur'">ɜːr</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span></span>-, -<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ɡ/: 'g' in 'guy'">ɡ</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span>-/</a></span><small class="nowrap metadata">&#160;(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:En-us-Bosnia_and_Herzegovina.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a>&#160;</span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/21/En-us-Bosnia_and_Herzegovina.ogg" class="internal" title="En-us-Bosnia and Herzegovina.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</small></span> or <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="'h' in 'hi'">h</span><span title="/ɜːr/: 'ur' in 'fur'">ɜːr</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/ɡ/: 'g' in 'guy'">ɡ</span><span title="/ɒ/: 'o' in 'body'">ɒ</span><span title="'v' in 'vie'">v</span><span title="/ɪ/: 'i' in 'kit'">ɪ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span></span>/</a></span></span>;<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> abbreviated <b>B&amp;H</b>; <a href="/wiki/Bosnian_language" title="Bosnian language">Bosnian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Serbian_language" title="Serbian language">Serbian</a>: <i>Bosna i Hercegovina (<b>BiH</b>)</i> / Боснa и Херцеговина (<b>БиХ</b>), <a href="/wiki/Croatian_language" title="Croatian language">Croatian</a>: <i>Bosna i Hercegovina (BiH)</i>, <small>pronounced&#160;</small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Serbo-Croatian" title="Help:IPA/Serbo-Croatian">[bôsna i xěrt͡seɡoʋina]</a></span>), sometimes called <b>Bosnia-Herzegovina</b>, and <a href="/wiki/Pars_pro_toto#Geography" title="Pars pro toto">often known informally as</a> <b>Bosnia</b>, is a country in <a href="/wiki/Southeastern_Europe" class="mw-redirect" title="Southeastern Europe">Southeastern Europe</a> located on the <a href="/wiki/Balkan_Peninsula" class="mw-redirect" title="Balkan Peninsula">Balkan Peninsula</a>. <a href="/wiki/Sarajevo" title="Sarajevo">Sarajevo</a> is the capital and largest city. Bordered by <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">Croatia</a> to the north, and west; <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a> to the east; <a href="/wiki/Montenegro" title="Montenegro">Montenegro</a> to the southeast; and the <a href="/wiki/Adriatic_Sea" title="Adriatic Sea">Adriatic Sea</a> to the south, with a coastline about 20 kilometres (12 miles) long surrounding the town of <a href="/wiki/Neum" title="Neum">Neum</a>. In the central and eastern interior of the country the geography is mountainous, in the northwest it is moderately hilly, and the northeast is predominantly flatland. The inland is a geographically larger region and has a moderate <a href="/wiki/Continental_climate" title="Continental climate">continental climate</a>, with hot summers and cold and snowy winters. The southern tip of the country has a <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_climate" title="Mediterranean climate">Mediterranean climate</a> and plain topography.</p>
<p>Bosnia and Herzegovina is a region that traces permanent human settlement back to the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> age, during and after which it was populated by several <a href="/wiki/Illyria" title="Illyria">Illyrian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">Celtic</a> civilizations. Culturally, politically, and socially, the country has a rich history, having been first settled by the <a href="/wiki/Slavic_peoples" class="mw-redirect" title="Slavic peoples">Slavic peoples</a> that populate the area today from the 6th through to the 9th centuries. In the 12th century the <a href="/wiki/Banate_of_Bosnia" title="Banate of Bosnia">Banate of Bosnia</a> was established, which evolved into the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Bosnia" title="Kingdom of Bosnia">Kingdom of Bosnia</a> in the 14th century, after which it was annexed into the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>, under whose rule it would remain from the mid-15th to the late 19th centuries. The <a href="/wiki/Ottomans" class="mw-redirect" title="Ottomans">Ottomans</a> brought <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Islam in Bosnia and Herzegovina">Islam</a> to the region, and altered much of the cultural and social outlook of the country. This was followed by <a href="/wiki/History_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina_(1878%E2%80%931918)" class="mw-redirect" title="History of Bosnia and Herzegovina (1878–1918)">annexation into the Austro-Hungarian Monarchy</a>, which lasted up until <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>. In the interwar period, Bosnia and Herzegovina was part of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Yugoslavia" title="Kingdom of Yugoslavia">Kingdom of Yugoslavia</a> and after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, it was granted full republic status in the newly formed <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Federal_Republic_of_Yugoslavia" title="Socialist Federal Republic of Yugoslavia">Socialist Federal Republic of Yugoslavia</a>. Following the dissolution of Yugoslavia, the republic <a href="/wiki/Breakup_of_Yugoslavia#Independence_of_the_Republic_of_Macedonia_and_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Breakup of Yugoslavia">proclaimed independence</a> in 1992, which was followed by the <a href="/wiki/Bosnian_War" title="Bosnian War">Bosnian War</a>, lasting until <a href="/wiki/Dayton_Agreement" title="Dayton Agreement">late 1995</a>.</p>
<p>Today, the country maintains high <a href="/wiki/List_of_countries_by_Human_Development_Index" title="List of countries by Human Development Index">literacy, life expectancy and education</a> levels and is one of the <a href="/wiki/Tourism_in_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Tourism in Bosnia and Herzegovina">most frequently visited countries in the region</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> projected to have the third highest tourism growth rate in the world between 1995 and 2020.<sup id="cite_ref-Newfound_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Newfound-13">[13]</a></sup> Bosnia and Herzegovina is known for its natural environment and <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Culture of Bosnia and Herzegovina">cultural heritage</a> inherited from six historical civilizations, its <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_and_Herzegovina_cuisine" title="Bosnia and Herzegovina cuisine">cuisine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tourism_in_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina#Winter_sports" title="Tourism in Bosnia and Herzegovina">winter sports</a>, its eclectic and unique <a href="/wiki/Music_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Music of Bosnia and Herzegovina">music</a>, <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Architecture of Bosnia and Herzegovina">architecture</a> and its <a href="/wiki/Sarajevo#Festivals" title="Sarajevo">festivals</a>, some of which are the largest and most prominent of their kind in Southeastern Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> The country is home to three main ethnic groups or, officially, <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups_in_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Ethnic groups in Bosnia and Herzegovina">constituent peoples</a>, as specified in the constitution. <a href="/wiki/Bosniaks" title="Bosniaks">Bosniaks</a> are the largest group of the three, with <a href="/wiki/Serbs_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Serbs of Bosnia and Herzegovina">Serbs</a> second and <a href="/wiki/Croats_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Croats of Bosnia and Herzegovina">Croats</a> third. A native of Bosnia and Herzegovina, regardless of ethnicity, is identified in English as a <a href="/wiki/Bosnians" title="Bosnians">Bosnian</a>. The terms <a href="/wiki/Herzegovinians" title="Herzegovinians">Herzegovinian</a> and Bosnian are maintained as a regional rather than ethnic distinction, and the region of Herzegovina has no precisely defined borders of its own. Moreover, the country was simply called "Bosnia" until the Austro-Hungarian occupation at the end of the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bosnia and Herzegovina has a <a href="/wiki/Bicameral" class="mw-redirect" title="Bicameral">bicameral</a> legislature and a three-member Presidency composed of a member of each major ethnic group. However, the central government's power is highly limited, as the country is largely decentralized and comprises two autonomous entities: the <a href="/wiki/Federation_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Federation of Bosnia and Herzegovina">Federation of Bosnia and Herzegovina</a> and <a href="/wiki/Republika_Srpska" title="Republika Srpska">Republika Srpska</a>, with a third region, the <a href="/wiki/Br%C4%8Dko_District" title="Brčko District">Brčko District</a>, governed under local government. The Federation of Bosnia and Herzegovina is itself complex and consists of 10 <a href="/wiki/Cantons_of_the_Federation_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Cantons of the Federation of Bosnia and Herzegovina">cantons</a>. The country is a <a href="/wiki/Accession_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina_to_the_European_Union" title="Accession of Bosnia and Herzegovina to the European Union">potential candidate for membership to the European Union</a> and has been a candidate for <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Treaty_Organisation" class="mw-redirect" title="North Atlantic Treaty Organisation">North Atlantic Treaty Organisation</a> membership since April 2010, when it received a <a href="/wiki/Membership_Action_Plan" class="mw-redirect" title="Membership Action Plan">Membership Action Plan</a> at a summit in <a href="/wiki/Tallinn" title="Tallinn">Tallinn</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Additionally, the country has been a member of the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Europe" title="Council of Europe">Council of Europe</a> since April 2002 and a founding member of the <a href="/wiki/Union_for_the_Mediterranean" title="Union for the Mediterranean">Mediterranean Union</a> upon its establishment in July 2008.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<p>The first preserved widely acknowledged mention of <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_(region)" title="Bosnia (region)">Bosnia</a> is in <i><a href="/wiki/De_Administrando_Imperio" title="De Administrando Imperio">De Administrando Imperio</a></i>, a politico-geographical handbook written by the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Byzantine_emperors" title="List of Byzantine emperors">Byzantine emperor</a> <a href="/wiki/Constantine_VII" title="Constantine VII">Constantine VII</a> in the mid-10th century (between 948 and 952) describing the "small land" (χωρίον in <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>) of "Bosona" (Βοσώνα).<sup id="cite_ref-Moravcsik-153_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moravcsik-153-18">[18]</a></sup> The name is believed to have been derived from the <a href="/wiki/Hydronym" title="Hydronym">hydronym</a> of the river <a href="/wiki/Bosna_(river)" title="Bosna (river)">Bosna</a> coursing through the Bosnian heartland. According to philologist Anton Mayer the name <i>Bosna</i> could be derived from <a href="/wiki/Illyrian_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Illyrian language">Illyrian</a> "Bass-an-as" which would be a diversion of the Proto-Indo-European root "bos" or "bogh", meaning "the running water".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002-19">[19]</a></sup> According to English medievalist <a href="/wiki/William_Miller_(historian)" title="William Miller (historian)">William Miller</a> the Slavic settlers in Bosnia "adapted the Latin designation [...] Basante, to their own idiom by calling the stream Bosna and themselves <i>Bosniaks</i> [...]".<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>The name <i>Herzegovina</i> ("herzog's [land]", from German word for "duke")<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002-19">[19]</a></sup> originates from Bosnian magnate <a href="/wiki/Stjepan_Vuk%C4%8Di%C4%87_Kosa%C4%8Da" title="Stjepan Vukčić Kosača">Stephen Vukčić Kosača</a>'s title, "Herceg (Herzog) of Hum and the Coast" (1448).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFine1994578_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFine1994578-21">[21]</a></sup> Hum, formerly <a href="/wiki/Zahumlje" class="mw-redirect" title="Zahumlje">Zahumlje</a>, was an early medieval principality that was conquered by the Bosnian Banate in the first half of the 14th century. The region was administered by the Ottomans as the <a href="/wiki/Sanjak_of_Herzegovina" title="Sanjak of Herzegovina">Sanjak of Herzegovina</a> (<i>Hersek</i>) within the <a href="/wiki/Eyalet_of_Bosnia" class="mw-redirect" title="Eyalet of Bosnia">Eyalet of Bosnia</a> up until the formation of the short-lived <a href="/wiki/Herzegovina_Eyalet" title="Herzegovina Eyalet">Herzegovina Eyalet</a> in the 1830s, which was remerged in the 1850s, after which the entity became commonly known as "Bosnia and Herzegovina".</p>
<p>On initial proclamation of independence in 1992, the country's official name was the <i><a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Republic of Bosnia and Herzegovina">Republic of Bosnia and Herzegovina</a></i> but following the 1995 <a href="/wiki/Dayton_Agreement" title="Dayton Agreement">Dayton Agreement</a> and the new constitution that accompanied it the name was officially changed to <i>Bosnia and Herzegovina</i>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="History of Bosnia and Herzegovina">History of Bosnia and Herzegovina</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_history">Early history</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mogorjelo_Villa_Rustica.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/ba/Mogorjelo_Villa_Rustica.jpg/220px-Mogorjelo_Villa_Rustica.jpg" width="220" height="139" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/ba/Mogorjelo_Villa_Rustica.jpg/330px-Mogorjelo_Villa_Rustica.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/ba/Mogorjelo_Villa_Rustica.jpg/440px-Mogorjelo_Villa_Rustica.jpg 2x" data-file-width="576" data-file-height="363" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mogorjelo_Villa_Rustica.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Mogorjelo" title="Mogorjelo">Mogorjelo</a>, ancient Roman suburban Villa Rustica from the 4th century, near <a href="/wiki/%C4%8Capljina" title="Čapljina">Čapljina</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Early_history_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Early history of Bosnia and Herzegovina">Early history of Bosnia and Herzegovina</a></div>
<p>Bosnia has been inhabited since at latest the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> age. The earliest Neolithic population became known in the <a href="/wiki/Classical_Antiquity" class="mw-redirect" title="Classical Antiquity">Antiquity</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Illyrians" title="Illyrians">Illyrians</a>. <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">Celtic</a> migrations in the 4th century&#160;BC were also notable. Concrete historical evidence for this period is scarce, but overall it appears that the region was populated by a number of different people speaking distinct languages. Conflict between the Illyrians and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Romans</a> started in 229&#160;BC, but Rome did not complete its annexation of the region until AD&#160;9. It was precisely in modern-day Bosnia and Herzegovina that Rome fought one of the most difficult battles in its history since the <a href="/wiki/Punic_Wars" title="Punic Wars">Punic Wars</a>, as described by the Roman historian <a href="/wiki/Suetonius" title="Suetonius">Suetonius</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> This was the Roman campaign against <a href="/wiki/Illyricum_(Roman_province)" title="Illyricum (Roman province)">Illyricum</a>, known as <i><a href="/wiki/Bellum_Batonianum" title="Bellum Batonianum">Bellum Batonianum</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> The conflict arose after an attempt to recruit Illyrians, and a revolt spanned for four years (6–9 AD), after which they were subdued.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> In the Roman period, Latin-speaking settlers from the entire <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> settled among the Illyrians, and Roman soldiers were encouraged to retire in the region.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002-19">[19]</a></sup> Following the split of the Empire between 337 and 395 AD, Dalmatia and Pannonia became parts of the <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">Western Roman Empire</a>. Some claim that the region was conquered by the <a href="/wiki/Ostrogoths" title="Ostrogoths">Ostrogoths</a> in 455 AD. It subsequently changed hands between the <a href="/wiki/Alans" title="Alans">Alans</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Huns" title="Huns">Huns</a>. By the 6th century, Emperor <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">Justinian</a> had reconquered the area for the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>. Slavs overwhelmed the Balkans in the 6th and 7th centuries. Illyrian cultural traits were adopted by the South Slavs, as evidenced in certain customs and traditions, placenames, etc.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Timothy Gregory explains:</p>
<div class="quotebox" style="margin: auto; padding: 10px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>"It is now generally agreed that the people who lived in the Balkans after the Slavic "invasions" were probably for the most part the same as those who had lived there earlier, although the creation of new political groups and arrival of small numbers of immigrants caused people to look at themselves as distinct from their neighbours, including the Byzantines".</div>
</div>
<cite style="display: block; text-align: left;">T E Gregory, <i>A History of Byzantium</i>. Wiley- Blackwell, 2010. Pg 169</cite></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Middle_Ages">Middle Ages</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_and_Herzegovina_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Bosnia and Herzegovina in the Middle Ages">Bosnia and Herzegovina in the Middle Ages</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Early_Slavs" title="Early Slavs">Early Slavs</a> raided the Western Balkans, including Bosnia, in the 6th and early 7th century (amid the <a href="/wiki/Migration_Period" title="Migration Period">Migration Period</a>), and were composed of small tribal units drawn from a single Slavic confederation known to the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantines</a> as the <i><a href="/wiki/Sclaveni" title="Sclaveni">Sclaveni</a></i> (whilst the related <i><a href="/wiki/Antes_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Antes people">Antes</a></i>, roughly speaking, colonized the eastern portions of the Balkans).<sup id="cite_ref-Columbia_University_Press_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Columbia_University_Press-26">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Tribes recorded by the ethnonyms of "Serb" and "Croat" are described as a second, latter, migration of different people during the second quarter of the 7th century who do not seem to have been particularly numerous;<sup id="cite_ref-Columbia_University_Press_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Columbia_University_Press-26">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFine199153_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFine199153-28">[28]</a></sup> these early "Serb" and "Croat" tribes, whose exact identity is subject to scholarly debate,<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> came to predominate over the Slavs in the neighbouring regions. The bulk of Bosnia proper, however, appears to have been a territory between Serb and Croat rule and is not enumerated as one of the regions settled by those tribes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFine199153_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFine199153-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bosnia is first mentioned <i>as a land (horion Bosona)</i> in Byzantine Emperor Constantine Porphyrogenitus' <i><a href="/wiki/De_Administrando_Imperio" title="De Administrando Imperio">De Administrando Imperio</a></i> in the mid 10th century, at the end of a chapter (Chap. 32) entitled "Of the Serbs and the country in which they now dwell".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBasic2009123_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBasic2009123-30">[30]</a></sup> This has been scholarly interpreted in several ways and used especially by the Serb national ideologists to prove Bosnia as originally a "Serb" land. Other scholars have asserted the inclusion of Bosnia into Chapter 32 to merely be the result of Serbian Grand Duke <a href="/wiki/%C4%8Caslav" title="Časlav">Časlav</a>'s temporary rule over Bosnia at the time, while also pointing out that Porphyrogenitus does not say anywhere explicitly that Bosnia is a "Serb land".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBasic2009123-28_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBasic2009123-28-31">[31]</a></sup> In fact, the very translation of the critical sentence in which the word <i>Bosona</i> (Bosnia) appears is subject to varying interpretation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBasic2009123_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBasic2009123-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>In time, Bosnia would come to form a unit under its own ruler calling himself Bosnian.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFine199153_28-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFine199153-28">[28]</a></sup> Bosnia, along with other territories, became part of <a href="/wiki/Duklja" title="Duklja">Duklja</a> in the 11th century, although it retained its own nobility and institutions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFine1991223_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFine1991223-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Medieval_Bosnian_State_Expansion-en.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Medieval_Bosnian_State_Expansion-en.svg/220px-Medieval_Bosnian_State_Expansion-en.svg.png" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Medieval_Bosnian_State_Expansion-en.svg/330px-Medieval_Bosnian_State_Expansion-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Medieval_Bosnian_State_Expansion-en.svg/440px-Medieval_Bosnian_State_Expansion-en.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="941" data-file-height="709" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Medieval_Bosnian_State_Expansion-en.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Bosnia in the Middle Ages spanning the <a href="/wiki/Banate_of_Bosnia" title="Banate of Bosnia">Banate of Bosnia</a> and the succeeding <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Bosnia" title="Kingdom of Bosnia">Kingdom of Bosnia</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/High_Middle_Ages" title="High Middle Ages">High Middle Ages</a> political circumstance led to the area being contested between the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hungary" title="Kingdom of Hungary">Kingdom of Hungary</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>. Following another shift of power between the two in the early 12th century, Bosnia found itself outside the control of both and emerged as the <a href="/wiki/Banate_of_Bosnia" title="Banate of Bosnia">Banate of Bosnia</a> (under the rule of local <i><a href="/wiki/Ban_(title)" title="Ban (title)">bans</a></i>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002_19-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002-19">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> The first Bosnian ban known by name was <a href="/wiki/Ban_Bori%C4%87" title="Ban Borić">Ban Borić</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFine1991288_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFine1991288-34">[34]</a></sup> The second was <a href="/wiki/Ban_Kulin" title="Ban Kulin">Ban Kulin</a> whose rule marked the start of a controversy involving the <a href="/wiki/Bosnian_Church" title="Bosnian Church">Bosnian Church</a> – considered heretical by the Roman Catholic Church. In response to Hungarian attempts to use church politics regarding the issue as a way to reclaim sovereignty over Bosnia, Kulin held a council of local church leaders to renounce the heresy and embraced Catholicism in 1203. Despite this, Hungarian ambitions remained unchanged long after Kulin's death in 1204, waning only after an unsuccessful invasion in 1254. During this time the population was called <i><a href="/wiki/Dobri_Bo%C5%A1njani" class="mw-redirect" title="Dobri Bošnjani">Dobri Bošnjani</a></i> ("Good Bosnians").<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> The names Serb and Croat, though occasionally appearing in peripheral areas, were not used in Bosnia proper.<sup id="cite_ref-lrb.co.uk_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lrb.co.uk-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bosnian history from then until the early 14th century was marked by a power struggle between the <a href="/wiki/%C5%A0ubi%C4%87" title="Šubić">Šubić</a> and <a href="/wiki/House_of_Kotromani%C4%87" class="mw-redirect" title="House of Kotromanić">Kotromanić</a> families. This conflict came to an end in 1322, when <a href="/wiki/Stephen_II,_Ban_of_Bosnia" title="Stephen II, Ban of Bosnia">Stephen II Kotromanić</a> became <i>Ban</i>. By the time of his death in 1353, he was successful in annexing territories to the north and west, as well as Zahumlje and parts of Dalmatia. He was succeeded by his ambitious nephew <a href="/wiki/Tvrtko_I_of_Bosnia" title="Tvrtko I of Bosnia">Tvrtko</a> who, following a prolonged struggle with nobility and inter-family strife, gained full control of the country in 1367. By the year 1377, Bosnia was elevated into a kingdom with the coronation of Tvrtko as the first <a href="/wiki/List_of_rulers_of_Bosnia" title="List of rulers of Bosnia">Bosnian King</a> in Mile near <a href="/wiki/Visoko_during_the_Middle_Ages" title="Visoko during the Middle Ages">Visoko</a> in the Bosnian heartland.<sup id="cite_ref-Mile_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mile-38">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-An_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-An-39">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following his death in 1391 however, Bosnia fell into a long period of decline. The <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> had already started its <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_wars_in_Europe" title="Ottoman wars in Europe">conquest of Europe</a> and posed a major threat to the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a> throughout the first half of the 15th century. Finally, after decades of political and social instability, the Kingdom of Bosnia ceased to exist in 1463 after its conquest by the Ottoman Empire.</p>
<h3><span id="Ottoman_Bosnia_(1463–1878)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Ottoman_Bosnia_.281463.E2.80.931878.29">Ottoman Bosnia (1463–1878)</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_conquest_of_Bosnia" class="mw-redirect" title="Ottoman conquest of Bosnia">Ottoman conquest of Bosnia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Bosnia" class="mw-redirect" title="Ottoman Bosnia">Ottoman Bosnia</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Begova_dzamija_(1).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Begova_dzamija_%281%29.jpg/220px-Begova_dzamija_%281%29.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Begova_dzamija_%281%29.jpg/330px-Begova_dzamija_%281%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Begova_dzamija_%281%29.jpg/440px-Begova_dzamija_%281%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1067" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Begova_dzamija_(1).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Gazi_Husrev-beg_Mosque" title="Gazi Husrev-beg Mosque">Gazi Husrev-beg Mosque</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sarajevo" title="Sarajevo">Sarajevo</a> dating from 1531</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Ottoman conquest of Bosnia marked a new era in the country's history and introduced drastic changes in the political and <a href="/wiki/Cultural_landscape" title="Cultural landscape">cultural landscape</a>. The Ottomans allowed for the preservation of Bosnia's identity by incorporating it as an integral province of the Ottoman Empire with its historical name and territorial integrity&#160;— a unique case among subjugated states in the Balkans.<sup id="cite_ref-Riedlmayer_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riedlmayer-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Within Bosnia the Ottomans introduced a number of key changes in the territory's socio-political administration; including a new landholding system, a reorganization of administrative units, and a complex system of social differentiation by class and religious affiliation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002_19-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>The four centuries of Ottoman rule also had a drastic impact on Bosnia's population make-up, which changed several times as a result of the empire's conquests, frequent wars with European powers, forced and economic migrations, and epidemics. A native Slavic-speaking Muslim community emerged and eventually became the largest of the ethno-religious groups due to lack of strong Christian church organizations and continuous rivalry between the Orthodox and Catholic churches, while the indigenous <a href="/wiki/Bosnian_Church" title="Bosnian Church">Bosnian Church</a> disappeared altogether (ostensibly by conversion of its members to Islam). The Ottomans referred to them as <i>kristianlar</i> while the Orthodox and Catholics were called <i>gebir</i> or <i>kafir</i>, meaning "unbeliever".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVelikonja200329.E2.80.9330_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVelikonja200329.E2.80.9330-42">[42]</a></sup> The Bosnian <a href="/wiki/Franciscan" class="mw-redirect" title="Franciscan">Franciscans</a> (and the Catholic population as a whole) were protected by official imperial decrees and in accordance and full extent of Ottoman laws, however in effect, these often merely affected arbitrary rule and behavior of powerful local elite.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002_19-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>As the Ottoman Empire continued their rule in the <a href="/wiki/Balkans" title="Balkans">Balkans</a> (<a href="/wiki/Rumelia" title="Rumelia">Rumelia</a>), Bosnia was somewhat relieved of the pressures of being a frontier province, and experienced a period of general welfare. A number of cities, such as Sarajevo and <a href="/wiki/Mostar" title="Mostar">Mostar</a>, were established and grew into regional centers of trade and <a href="/wiki/Urban_culture" title="Urban culture">urban culture</a> and were then visited by <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> traveler <a href="/wiki/Evliya_%C3%87elebi" title="Evliya Çelebi">Evliya Çelebi</a> in 1648. Within these cities, various Ottoman Sultans financed the construction of many works of <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Architecture of Bosnia and Herzegovina">Bosnian architecture</a> such as the country's first library in <a href="/wiki/Sarajevo" title="Sarajevo">Sarajevo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Madrassa" class="mw-redirect" title="Madrassa">madrassas</a>, a school of <a href="/wiki/Sufi_philosophy" title="Sufi philosophy">Sufi philosophy</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/Clock_tower" title="Clock tower">clock tower</a> (<i>Sahat Kula</i>), bridges such as the <a href="/wiki/Stari_Most" title="Stari Most">Stari Most</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Tsar%27s_Mosque" class="mw-redirect" title="Tsar's Mosque">Tsar's Mosque</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Gazi_Husrev-beg%27s_Mosque" class="mw-redirect" title="Gazi Husrev-beg's Mosque">Gazi Husrev-beg's Mosque</a>.</p>
<p>Furthermore, several Bosnian Muslims played influential roles in the Ottoman Empire's cultural and <a href="/wiki/Political_history" title="Political history">political history</a> during this time.<sup id="cite_ref-Riedlmayer_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riedlmayer-41">[41]</a></sup> Bosnian recruits formed a large component of the Ottoman ranks in the battles of <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Moh%C3%A1cs" title="Battle of Mohács">Mohács</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Krbava_field" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Krbava field">Krbava field</a>, while numerous other Bosnians rose through the ranks of the Ottoman military to occupy the highest positions of power in the Empire, including admirals such as <a href="/wiki/Matrak%C3%A7%C4%B1_Nasuh" title="Matrakçı Nasuh">Matrakçı Nasuh</a>; generals such as <a href="/wiki/Isa-Beg_Isakovi%C4%87" class="mw-redirect" title="Isa-Beg Isaković">Isa-Beg Isaković</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gazi_Husrev-beg" title="Gazi Husrev-beg">Gazi Husrev-beg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hasan_Predojevi%C4%87" class="mw-redirect" title="Hasan Predojević">Hasan Predojević</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sar%C4%B1_S%C3%BCleyman_Pa%C5%9Fa" class="mw-redirect" title="Sarı Süleyman Paşa">Sarı Süleyman Paşa</a>; administrators such as <a href="/wiki/Ferhat-pa%C5%A1a_Sokolovi%C4%87" class="mw-redirect" title="Ferhat-paša Sokolović">Ferhat-paša Sokolović</a> and <a href="/wiki/Osman_Grada%C5%A1%C4%8Devi%C4%87" title="Osman Gradaščević">Osman Gradaščević</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Vizier" title="Vizier">Grand Viziers</a> such as the influential <a href="/wiki/Mehmed_Pa%C5%A1a_Sokolovi%C4%87" class="mw-redirect" title="Mehmed Paša Sokolović">Mehmed Paša Sokolović</a> and <a href="/wiki/Damad_Ibrahim_Pasha" class="mw-redirect" title="Damad Ibrahim Pasha">Damad Ibrahim Pasha</a>. Some Bosnians emerged as <a href="/wiki/Sufi" class="mw-redirect" title="Sufi">Sufi</a> mystics, scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Muhamed_Hevaji_Uskufi_Bosnevi" title="Muhamed Hevaji Uskufi Bosnevi">Muhamed Hevaji Uskufi Bosnevi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ali_D%C5%BEabi%C4%8D" class="mw-redirect" title="Ali Džabič">Ali Džabič</a>; and poets in the <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Albanian_language" title="Albanian language">Albanian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian languages</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Imamovi.C4.87.2C_Mustafa_1996_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Imamovi.C4.87.2C_Mustafa_1996-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sarajevo_1878..jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Sarajevo_1878..jpg/220px-Sarajevo_1878..jpg" width="220" height="159" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Sarajevo_1878..jpg/330px-Sarajevo_1878..jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Sarajevo_1878..jpg/440px-Sarajevo_1878..jpg 2x" data-file-width="716" data-file-height="518" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sarajevo_1878..jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Austro-Hungarian troops <a href="/wiki/Austro-Hungarian_campaign_in_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina_in_1878" title="Austro-Hungarian campaign in Bosnia and Herzegovina in 1878">enter Sarajevo</a>, 1878</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>However, by the late 17th century the Empire's military misfortunes caught up with the country, and the conclusion of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Turkish_War" title="Great Turkish War">Great Turkish War</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Karlowitz" title="Treaty of Karlowitz">treaty of Karlowitz</a> in 1699 once again made Bosnia the Empire's westernmost province. The following century was marked by further military failures, numerous revolts within Bosnia, and several outbursts of plague. The Porte's efforts at modernizing the Ottoman state were met with distrust growing to hostility in Bosnia, where local aristocrats stood to lose much through the proposed reforms.</p>
<p>This, combined with frustrations over territorial, political concessions in the north-east, and the plight of <a href="/wiki/Slavic_peoples" class="mw-redirect" title="Slavic peoples">Slavic</a> <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> refugees arriving from the <a href="/wiki/Sanjak_of_Smederevo" title="Sanjak of Smederevo">Sanjak of Smederevo</a> into <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_Eyalet" title="Bosnia Eyalet">Bosnia Eyalet</a>, culminated in a partially unsuccessful revolt by <a href="/wiki/Husein_Grada%C5%A1%C4%8Devi%C4%87" title="Husein Gradaščević">Husein Gradaščević</a>, who endorsed a multicultural <a href="/wiki/Bosnia_Eyalet" title="Bosnia Eyalet">Bosnia Eyalet</a> autonomous from the authoritarian rule of the Ottoman Sultan <a href="/wiki/Mahmud_II" title="Mahmud II">Mahmud II</a>, who persecuted, executed and abolished the <a href="/wiki/Janissaries" title="Janissaries">Janissaries</a> and reduced the role of autonomous <a href="/wiki/Pasha" title="Pasha">Pashas</a> in <a href="/wiki/Rumelia" title="Rumelia">Rumelia</a>. Mahmud II sent his <a href="/wiki/Grand_Vizier" class="mw-redirect" title="Grand Vizier">Grand Vizier</a> to subdue Bosnia Eyalet and succeeded only with the reluctant assistance of <a href="/wiki/Ali-pa%C5%A1a_Rizvanbegovi%C4%87" title="Ali-paša Rizvanbegović">Ali-paša Rizvanbegović</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Imamovi.C4.87.2C_Mustafa_1996_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Imamovi.C4.87.2C_Mustafa_1996-43">[43]</a></sup> Related rebellions would be extinguished by 1850, but the situation continued to deteriorate. Later agrarian unrest eventually sparked the <a href="/wiki/Herzegovina_Uprising_(1875-1878)" class="mw-redirect" title="Herzegovina Uprising (1875-1878)">Herzegovinian rebellion</a>, a widespread peasant uprising, in 1875. The conflict rapidly spread and came to involve several Balkan states and Great Powers, a situation which eventually led to the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Berlin" title="Congress of Berlin">Congress of Berlin</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Berlin_(1878)" title="Treaty of Berlin (1878)">Treaty of Berlin</a> in 1878.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002_19-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Austro-Hungarian_rule_(1878–1918)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Austro-Hungarian_rule_.281878.E2.80.931918.29">Austro-Hungarian rule (1878–1918)</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina_(1878%E2%80%931918)" class="mw-redirect" title="History of Bosnia and Herzegovina (1878–1918)">History of Bosnia and Herzegovina (1878–1918)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sarajevo,_Academy_of_Arts.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Sarajevo%2C_Academy_of_Arts.jpg/220px-Sarajevo%2C_Academy_of_Arts.jpg" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Sarajevo%2C_Academy_of_Arts.jpg/330px-Sarajevo%2C_Academy_of_Arts.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Sarajevo%2C_Academy_of_Arts.jpg/440px-Sarajevo%2C_Academy_of_Arts.jpg 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="1200" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sarajevo,_Academy_of_Arts.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Dome and towers on the Academy of Arts in Sarajevo, designed by the Czech-born architect <a href="/wiki/Karel_Pa%C5%99%C3%ADk" title="Karel Pařík">Karel Pařík</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At the <a href="/wiki/Congress_of_Berlin" title="Congress of Berlin">Congress of Berlin</a> in 1878, the <a href="/wiki/Austria-Hungary" title="Austria-Hungary">Austro-Hungarian</a> Foreign Minister <a href="/wiki/Gyula_Andr%C3%A1ssy" title="Gyula Andrássy">Gyula Andrássy</a> obtained the occupation and administration of Bosnia and Herzegovina, and he also obtained the right to station garrisons in the <a href="/wiki/Sanjak_of_Novi_Pazar" title="Sanjak of Novi Pazar">Sanjak of Novi Pazar</a>, which remained under <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> administration. The Sanjak preserved the separation of Serbia and Montenegro, and the Austro-Hungarian garrisons there would open the way for a dash to <a href="/wiki/Salonika" class="mw-redirect" title="Salonika">Salonika</a> that "would bring the western half of the Balkans under permanent Austrian influence."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlbertini195219_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlbertini195219-44">[44]</a></sup> "High [Austro-Hungarian] military authorities desired [an...] immediate major expedition with Salonika as its objective."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlbertini195233_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlbertini195233-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote>
<p>On 28 September 1878, the Finance Minister, Koloman von Zell, threatened to resign if the army, backed by the <a href="/wiki/Archduke_Albrecht,_Duke_of_Teschen" title="Archduke Albrecht, Duke of Teschen">Archduke Albert</a>, were allowed to advance to Salonika. In the session of the Hungarian Parliament of 5 November 1878 the Opposition proposed that the Foreign Minister should be impeached for violating the constitution with his policy during the Near East Crisis and by the occupation of Bosnia-Herzegovina. The motion lost 179 to 95. The gravest accusations were raised by the opposition rank and file against Andrassy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlbertini195233_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlbertini195233-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Although an Austro-Hungarian side quickly came to an agreement with Bosnians, tensions remained in certain parts of the country (particularly the south) and a mass emigration of predominantly Slavic dissidents occurred.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002_19-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002-19">[19]</a></sup> However, a state of relative stability was reached soon enough and Austro-Hungarian authorities were able to embark on a number of social and administrative reforms which intended to make Bosnia and Herzegovina into a "model colony".</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sarajevo_Tram_(1901).png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Sarajevo_Tram_%281901%29.png/220px-Sarajevo_Tram_%281901%29.png" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Sarajevo_Tram_%281901%29.png/330px-Sarajevo_Tram_%281901%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Sarajevo_Tram_%281901%29.png/440px-Sarajevo_Tram_%281901%29.png 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="670" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sarajevo_Tram_(1901).png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Sarajevo_Tramway" class="mw-redirect" title="Sarajevo Tramway">Sarajevo Tramway</a> in 1901</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>With the aim of establishing the province as a stable <a href="/wiki/Political_spectrum" title="Political spectrum">political model</a> that would help dissipate rising South Slav nationalism, Habsburg rule did much to codify laws, to introduce new political practices, and to provide for modernisation. The Austro-Hungarian Empire built the three Roman Catholic churches in Sarajevo and these three churches are among only 20 Catholic churches in the state of Bosnia.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (August 2014)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata">– <a href="/wiki/Talk:Bosnia_and_Herzegovina#Dubious" title="Talk:Bosnia and Herzegovina">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In 1881, within three years of formal occupation of Bosnia Herzegovina, Austria-Hungary obtained German and the more important Russian approval of the annexation of these provinces at a time that suited Vienna. This mandate was formally ratified by the <a href="/wiki/League_of_the_Three_Emperors" title="League of the Three Emperors">Dreikaiserbund (Three Emperor's Treaty)</a> on 18 June of that year.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlbertini200537_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlbertini200537-46">[46]</a></sup> Upon the accession of Czar Nicholas II, however, the Russians reneged on the agreement, asserting in 1897 the need for special scrutiny of the Bosnian Annexation issue at an unspecified future date.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlbertini200594_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlbertini200594-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>External matters began to affect the Bosnian Protectorate, however, and its relationship with Austria-Hungary. A bloody coup occurred in Serbia, on 10 June 1903, which brought a radical anti-Austrian government into power in Belgrade.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlbertini2005140_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlbertini2005140-48">[48]</a></sup> Also, the revolt in the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> in 1908, raised concerns that the Istanbul government might seek the outright return of Bosnia Herzegovina. These factors caused the Austrian-Hungarian government to seek a permanent resolution of the Bosnian question sooner, rather than later.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:National_Museum_of_BiH_Aerial.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/National_Museum_of_BiH_Aerial.JPG/220px-National_Museum_of_BiH_Aerial.JPG" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/National_Museum_of_BiH_Aerial.JPG/330px-National_Museum_of_BiH_Aerial.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/National_Museum_of_BiH_Aerial.JPG/440px-National_Museum_of_BiH_Aerial.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2800" data-file-height="1867" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:National_Museum_of_BiH_Aerial.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="National Museum of Bosnia and Herzegovina">National Museum of Bosnia and Herzegovina</a> was established in 1888</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On 2 July 1908, in response to the pressing of the Austrian-Hungarian claim, the Russian Imperial Foreign Minister Alexander Izvolsky offered to support the Bosnian annexation in return for Vienna's support for Russia's bid for naval access through the <a href="/wiki/Dardanelles" title="Dardanelles">Dardanelles</a> Straits into the Mediterranean.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlbertini2005195_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlbertini2005195-49">[49]</a></sup> With the Russians being, at least, provisionally willing to keep their word over Bosnia Herzegovina for the first time in 11 years, Austria-Hungary waited and then published the annexation proclamation on 6 October 1908. The international furor over the annexation announcement caused Izvolsky to drop the Dardanelles Straits question, altogether, in an effort to obtain a European conference over the Bosnian Annexation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAlbertini2005227_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAlbertini2005227-50">[50]</a></sup> This conference never materialized and without British or French support, the Russians and their client state, Serbia, were compelled to accept the Austrian-Hungarian annexation of Bosnia Herzegovina in March 1909.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DC-1914-27-d-Sarajevo-cropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/DC-1914-27-d-Sarajevo-cropped.jpg/220px-DC-1914-27-d-Sarajevo-cropped.jpg" width="220" height="249" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/DC-1914-27-d-Sarajevo-cropped.jpg/330px-DC-1914-27-d-Sarajevo-cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/DC-1914-27-d-Sarajevo-cropped.jpg/440px-DC-1914-27-d-Sarajevo-cropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="1737" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DC-1914-27-d-Sarajevo-cropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Assassination of <a href="/wiki/Archduke_Franz_Ferdinand_of_Austria" title="Archduke Franz Ferdinand of Austria">Archduke Franz Ferdinand of Austria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sophie,_Duchess_of_Hohenberg" title="Sophie, Duchess of Hohenberg">Sophie, Duchess of Hohenberg</a> in Sarajevo, illustrated in the Italian newspaper <a href="/wiki/Domenica_del_Corriere" class="mw-redirect" title="Domenica del Corriere">Domenica del Corriere</a>, 12 July 1914 by <a href="/wiki/Achille_Beltrame" title="Achille Beltrame">Achille Beltrame</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Political tensions culminated on 28 June 1914, when a Yugoslav nationalist youth named <a href="/wiki/Gavrilo_Princip" title="Gavrilo Princip">Gavrilo Princip</a>, a member of the secret Serbian-supported movement, <a href="/wiki/Young_Bosnia" title="Young Bosnia">Young Bosnia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Archduke_Franz_Ferdinand_of_Austria" title="Assassination of Archduke Franz Ferdinand of Austria">assassinated</a> the heir to the Austro-Hungarian throne, <a href="/wiki/Archduke_Franz_Ferdinand_of_Austria" title="Archduke Franz Ferdinand of Austria">Archduke Franz Ferdinand</a>, in Sarajevo—an event that proved to be the spark that set off World War I. At the end of the war, the <a href="/wiki/Bosniaks" title="Bosniaks">Bosniaks</a> had lost more men per capita than any other ethnic group in the Habsburg Empire whilst serving in the <a href="/wiki/Bosnian-Herzegovinian_Infantry" title="Bosnian-Herzegovinian Infantry">Bosnian-Herzegovinian Infantry</a> (known as <i>Bosniaken</i>) of the <a href="/wiki/Austro-Hungarian_Army" title="Austro-Hungarian Army">Austro-Hungarian Army</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Schachinger_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Schachinger-51">[51]</a></sup> Nonetheless, Bosnia and Herzegovina as a whole managed to escape the conflict relatively unscathed.<sup id="cite_ref-Riedlmayer_41-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riedlmayer-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Austro-Hungarian authorities established an auxiliary militia known as the <a href="/wiki/Schutzkorps" title="Schutzkorps">Schutzkorps</a> with a moot role in the empire's policy of <a href="/wiki/Anti-Serb" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-Serb">anti-Serb</a> repression.<sup id="cite_ref-Banac1988_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Banac1988-52">[52]</a></sup> Schutzkorps, predominantly recruited among the Muslim (Bosniak) population, were tasked with hunting down rebel Serbs (the <i><a href="/wiki/Chetniks" title="Chetniks">Chetniks</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Komiti" class="mw-redirect" title="Komiti">Komiti</a></i>)<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVelikonja2003141_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVelikonja2003141-53">[53]</a></sup> and became known for their persecution of <a href="/wiki/Serbs" title="Serbs">Serbs</a> particularly in Serb populated areas of eastern Bosnia, where they partly retaliated against Serbian <a href="/wiki/Chetniks" title="Chetniks">Chetniks</a> who in fall 1914 had carried out attacks against the Muslim population in the area.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> The proceedings of the Austro-Hungarian authorities led to around 5,500 citizens of Serb ethnicity in Bosnia and Herzegovina being arrested, and between 700 and 2,200 died in prison while 460 were executed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVelikonja2003141_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVelikonja2003141-53">[53]</a></sup> Around 5,200 Serb families were forcibly expelled from Bosnia and Herzegovina.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVelikonja2003141_53-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVelikonja2003141-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Kingdom_of_Yugoslavia_(1918–1941)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Kingdom_of_Yugoslavia_.281918.E2.80.931941.29">Kingdom of Yugoslavia (1918–1941)</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina_(1918%E2%80%931941)" class="mw-redirect" title="History of Bosnia and Herzegovina (1918–1941)">History of Bosnia and Herzegovina (1918–1941)</a></div>
<p>Following World War I, Bosnia and Herzegovina joined the South Slav <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Yugoslavia" title="Kingdom of Yugoslavia">Kingdom of Serbs, Croats and Slovenes</a> (soon renamed Yugoslavia). Political life in Bosnia at this time was marked by two major trends: social and economic unrest over <a href="/wiki/Redistribution_(economics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Redistribution (economics)">property redistribution</a>, and formation of several political parties that frequently changed coalitions and alliances with parties in other Yugoslav regions.<sup id="cite_ref-Riedlmayer_41-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Riedlmayer-41">[41]</a></sup> The dominant ideological conflict of the Yugoslav state, between Croatian regionalism and Serbian centralization, was approached differently by Bosnia's major <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_group" title="Ethnic group">ethnic groups</a> and was dependent on the overall political atmosphere.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002_19-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002-19">[19]</a></sup> The political reforms brought about in the newly established Yugoslavian kingdom saw few benefits for the Bosniaks; according to the 1910 final census of land ownership and population according to religious affiliation conducted in Austro-Hungary, Muslims (Bosniaks) owned 91.1%, Orthodox Serbians owned 6.0%, Croatian Catholics owned 2.6% and others, 0.3% of the property. Following the reforms Bosnian Muslims had a total of 1,175,305 hectares of agricultural and forest land taken away from them.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>Although the initial split of the country into 33 <a href="/wiki/Oblast" title="Oblast">oblasts</a> erased the presence of traditional geographic entities from the map, the efforts of Bosnian politicians such as <a href="/wiki/Mehmed_Spaho" title="Mehmed Spaho">Mehmed Spaho</a> ensured that the six oblasts carved up from Bosnia and Herzegovina corresponded to the six sanjaks from Ottoman times and, thus, matched the country's traditional boundary as a whole.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002_19-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>The establishment of the Kingdom of Yugoslavia in 1929, however, brought the redrawing of administrative regions into <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Yugoslavia#Subdivisions" title="Kingdom of Yugoslavia">banates</a> or <i><a href="/wiki/Banovina_(region)" title="Banovina (region)">banovinas</a></i> that purposely avoided all historical and ethnic lines, removing any trace of a Bosnian entity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002_19-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002-19">[19]</a></sup> Serbo-Croat tensions over the structuring of the Yugoslav state continued, with the concept of a separate Bosnian division receiving little or no consideration.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Cvetkovi%C4%87%E2%80%93Ma%C4%8Dek_Agreement" title="Cvetković–Maček Agreement">Cvetković-Maček Agreement</a> that created the <a href="/wiki/Banovina_of_Croatia" title="Banovina of Croatia">Croatian banate</a> in 1939 encouraged what was essentially a <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_Bosnia" class="mw-redirect" title="Partition of Bosnia">partition of Bosnia</a> between Croatia and Serbia.<sup id="cite_ref-Imamovi.C4.87.2C_Mustafa_1996_43-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Imamovi.C4.87.2C_Mustafa_1996-43">[43]</a></sup> However the rising threat of <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a> forced Yugoslav politicians to shift their attention. Following a period that saw attempts at <a href="/wiki/Appeasement" title="Appeasement">appeasement</a>, the signing of the <a href="/wiki/Tripartite_Pact" title="Tripartite Pact">Tripartite Treaty</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/Yugoslav_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="Yugoslav coup d'état">coup d'état</a>, Yugoslavia was finally invaded by Germany on 6 April 1941.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002_19-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="World_War_II_(1941–45)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="World_War_II_.281941.E2.80.9345.29">World War II (1941–45)</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina_(1941%E2%80%931945)" class="mw-redirect" title="History of Bosnia and Herzegovina (1941–1945)">History of Bosnia and Herzegovina (1941–1945)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Neretva_most.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Neretva_most.jpg/220px-Neretva_most.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Neretva_most.jpg/330px-Neretva_most.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Neretva_most.jpg/440px-Neretva_most.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="685" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Neretva_most.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The railway bridge over the <a href="/wiki/Neretva" title="Neretva">Neretva river</a> in <a href="/wiki/Jablanica,_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Jablanica, Bosnia and Herzegovina">Jablanica</a>, twice destroyed during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Neretva" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of the Neretva">Battle of the Neretva</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Once the kingdom of Yugoslavia was conquered by Nazi forces in World War II, all of Bosnia was ceded to the Nazi puppet regime, the <a href="/wiki/Independent_State_of_Croatia" title="Independent State of Croatia">Independent State of Croatia</a> (NDH). The NDH leaders embarked on a <a href="/wiki/World_War_II_persecution_of_Serbs" title="World War II persecution of Serbs">campaign of extermination</a> of <a href="/wiki/Serbs" title="Serbs">Serbs</a>, Jews, <a href="/wiki/Romani_people" title="Romani people">Romani</a>, Croats who opposed the regime, communists and large numbers of <a href="/wiki/Josip_Broz_Tito" title="Josip Broz Tito">Josip Broz Tito</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Yugoslav_Partisans" title="Yugoslav Partisans">Partisans</a> by setting up a number of <a href="/wiki/Jasenovac_concentration_camp" title="Jasenovac concentration camp">death camps</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> An estimated 209,000 Serbs and Montenegrins were killed on the territory of Bosnia-Herzegovina during the war.<sup id="cite_ref-Cohen_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (December 2015)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata">– <a href="/wiki/Talk:Bosnia_and_Herzegovina#The_figure_includes_the_total_number_of_Serb_casualties_whether_civilian_or_combatant_and_does_not_specify_perpetrating_faction._A_figure_detailing_the_number_of_Serb_civilian_deaths_attributable_to_the_NDH_only_would_be_preferred_in_this_regard" title="Talk:Bosnia and Herzegovina">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup> The <a href="/wiki/Usta%C5%A1e" title="Ustaše">Ustaše</a> recognized both Roman Catholicism and <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> as the national religions, but held the position that <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodoxy" class="mw-redirect" title="Eastern Orthodoxy">Eastern Orthodoxy</a>, as a symbol of Serbian identity, was their greatest foe.<sup id="cite_ref-Ramet.2C_p._118_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ramet.2C_p._118-59">[59]</a></sup> Although Croats were by far the largest ethnic group to constitute the Ustaše, the Vice President of the NDH and leader of the Yugoslav Muslim Organization <a href="/wiki/D%C5%BEafer_Kulenovi%C4%87" title="Džafer Kulenović">Džafer Kulenović</a> was a Muslim, and Muslims (Bosniaks) in total comprised nearly 12% of the Ustaše military and civil service authority.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVelikonja2003179_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVelikonja2003179-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many Serbs themselves took up arms and joined the <a href="/wiki/Chetniks" title="Chetniks">Chetniks</a>, a Serb nationalist movement with the aim of establishing an ethnically homogeneous '<a href="/wiki/Greater_Serbia" title="Greater Serbia">Greater Serbian</a>' state.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> The Chetniks were responsible for widespread persecution and murder of non-Serbs and communist sympathizers, with the Muslim population of Bosnia, Herzegovina and <a href="/wiki/Sand%C5%BEak" title="Sandžak">Sandžak</a> being a primary target.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Once captured, Muslim villagers were systematically massacred by the Chetniks.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Of the 75,000 Muslims who lost their lives in Bosnia and Herzegovina during the war,<sup id="cite_ref-Cohen_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen-58">[58]</a></sup> approximately 30,000 (mostly civilians) were killed by the Chetniks.<sup id="cite_ref-Geiger_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geiger-64">[64]</a></sup> Later, a number of Muslims served in Nazi <a href="/wiki/13th_Waffen_Mountain_Division_of_the_SS_Handschar_(1st_Croatian)" title="13th Waffen Mountain Division of the SS Handschar (1st Croatian)"><i>Waffen-SS</i></a> units.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Between 64,000 and 79,000 Bosnian Croats also perished from April 1941 to May 1945.<sup id="cite_ref-Cohen_58-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen-58">[58]</a></sup> Of these, about 18,000 were killed by the Chetniks.<sup id="cite_ref-Geiger_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Geiger-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Eternal_flame_in_Sarajevo_(2).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/The_Eternal_flame_in_Sarajevo_%282%29.JPG/220px-The_Eternal_flame_in_Sarajevo_%282%29.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/The_Eternal_flame_in_Sarajevo_%282%29.JPG/330px-The_Eternal_flame_in_Sarajevo_%282%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/The_Eternal_flame_in_Sarajevo_%282%29.JPG/440px-The_Eternal_flame_in_Sarajevo_%282%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3456" data-file-height="2592" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_Eternal_flame_in_Sarajevo_(2).JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Eternal_flame_(Sarajevo)" title="Eternal flame (Sarajevo)">Eternal flame</a> memorial to the military and civilian <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> victims</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On 12 October 1941, a group of 108 notable Muslim citizens of Sarajevo signed the <a href="/wiki/Resolution_of_Sarajevo_Muslims" title="Resolution of Sarajevo Muslims">Resolution of Sarajevo Muslims</a> by which they condemned the persecution of Serbs organized by the <a href="/wiki/Usta%C5%A1e" title="Ustaše">Ustaše</a>, made distinction between Muslims who participated in such persecutions and whole Muslim population, presented information about the persecutions of Muslims by Serbs and requested security for all citizens of the country, regardless of their identity.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>Starting in 1941, Yugoslav communists under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Josip_Broz_Tito" title="Josip Broz Tito">Josip Broz Tito</a> organized their own multi-ethnic resistance group, the <a href="/wiki/Partisans_(Yugoslavia)" class="mw-redirect" title="Partisans (Yugoslavia)">partisans</a>, who fought against both Axis and Chetnik forces. On 29 November 1943 the <a href="/wiki/Anti-Fascist_Council_of_National_Liberation_of_Yugoslavia" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-Fascist Council of National Liberation of Yugoslavia">Anti-Fascist Council of National Liberation of Yugoslavia</a> with Tito at its helm held a founding conference in <a href="/wiki/Jajce" title="Jajce">Jajce</a> where Bosnia and Herzegovina was reestablished as a republic within the Yugoslavian federation in its Habsburg borders.</p>
<p>Military success eventually prompted the Allies to support the Partisans, but Tito declined their offer to help and relied on his own forces instead. All the major military offensives by the antifascist movement of Yugoslavia against Nazis and their local supporters were conducted in Bosnia-Herzegovina and its peoples bore the brunt of fighting. More than 300,000 people died in Bosnia and Herzegovina in World War II.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> At the end of the war the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Federal_Republic_of_Yugoslavia" title="Socialist Federal Republic of Yugoslavia">Socialist Federal Republic of Yugoslavia</a>, with the <a href="/wiki/1946_Yugoslav_Constitution" title="1946 Yugoslav Constitution">constitution of 1946</a>, officially made Bosnia and Herzegovina one of six constituent republics in the new state.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002_19-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Socialist_Federative_Republic_of_Yugoslavia_(1945–1992)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Socialist_Federative_Republic_of_Yugoslavia_.281945.E2.80.931992.29">Socialist Federative Republic of Yugoslavia (1945–1992)</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina_(1945%E2%80%931992)" class="mw-redirect" title="History of Bosnia and Herzegovina (1945–1992)">History of Bosnia and Herzegovina (1945–1992)</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Republic_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Socialist Republic of Bosnia and Herzegovina">Socialist Republic of Bosnia and Herzegovina</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_SR_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Flag_of_SR_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina.svg/220px-Flag_of_SR_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina.svg.png" width="220" height="110" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Flag_of_SR_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina.svg/330px-Flag_of_SR_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Flag_of_SR_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina.svg/440px-Flag_of_SR_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_SR_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Bosnia and Herzegovina's flag while in the <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Federative_Republic_of_Yugoslavia" class="mw-redirect" title="Socialist Federative Republic of Yugoslavia">Socialist Federative Republic of Yugoslavia</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Due to its central geographic position within the Yugoslavian federation, post-war Bosnia was selected as a base for the development of the <a href="/wiki/Defense_(military)" class="mw-redirect" title="Defense (military)">military defense</a> industry. This contributed to a large concentration of arms and military personnel in Bosnia; a significant factor in <a href="/wiki/Bosnian_War" title="Bosnian War">the war that followed the break-up of Yugoslavia</a> in the 1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002_19-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMalcolm2002-19">[19]</a></sup> However, Bosnia's existence within Yugoslavia, for the large part, was a peaceful and very prosperous country, with high employment, a strong industrial and export oriented economy, good education system and social and medical security for every citizen of S. R. Bosnia and Herzegovina. Several international corporations operated in Bosnia— <a href="/wiki/Volkswagen" title="Volkswagen">Volkswagen</a> (car factory in Sarajevo, from 1972), <a href="/wiki/Coca-Cola" title="Coca-Cola">Coca-Cola</a> (from 1975), SKF Sweden (from 1967), Marlboro, (a tobacco factory in Sarajevo), and <a href="/wiki/Holiday_Inn" title="Holiday Inn">Holiday Inn</a> hotels. Sarajevo was the site of the <a href="/wiki/1984_Winter_Olympics" title="1984 Winter Olympics">1984 Winter Olympics</a>.</p>
<p>During the 1950s and 1960s Bosnia was a political backwater of the Republic of Yugoslavia. In the 1970s a strong Bosnian political elite arose, fueled in part by Tito's leadership in the <a href="/wiki/Non-Aligned_Movement" title="Non-Aligned Movement">Non-Aligned Movement</a> and Bosnians serving in Yugoslavia's <a href="/wiki/Diplomatic_corps" title="Diplomatic corps">diplomatic corps</a>. While working within the Socialist system, politicians such as <a href="/wiki/D%C5%BEemal_Bijedi%C4%87" title="Džemal Bijedić">Džemal Bijedić</a>, <a href="/wiki/Branko_Mikuli%C4%87" title="Branko Mikulić">Branko Mikulić</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hamdija_Pozderac" title="Hamdija Pozderac">Hamdija Pozderac</a> reinforced and protected the sovereignty of Bosnia and Herzegovina.<sup id="cite_ref-Stojic_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stojic-68">[68]</a></sup> Their efforts proved key during the turbulent period following Tito's death in 1980, and are today considered some of the early steps towards Bosnian independence. However, the republic did not escape the increasingly nationalistic climate of the time. With the fall of the Soviet Union and the start of the break-up of Yugoslavia, doctrine of tolerance began to lose its potency, creating an opportunity for nationalist elements in the society to spread their influence.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span id="Bosnian_War_(1992–1995)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Bosnian_War_.281992.E2.80.931995.29">Bosnian War (1992–1995)</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bosnian_War" title="Bosnian War">Bosnian War</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Bosnia_and_Herzegovina" title="Republic of Bosnia and Herzegovina">Republic of Bosnia and Herzegovina</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Evstafiev-sarajevo-building-burns.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Evstafiev-sarajevo-building-burns.jpg/220px-Evstafiev-sarajevo-building-burns.jpg" width="220" height="288" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Evstafiev-sarajevo-building-burns.jpg/330px-Evstafiev-sarajevo-building-burns.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/02/Evstafiev-sarajevo-building-burns.jpg 2x" data-file-width="386" data-file-height="506" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Evstafiev-sarajevo-building-burns.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Parliament of Bosnia and Herzegovina burns after being struck by tank fire during the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Sarajevo" title="Siege of Sarajevo">Siege of Sarajevo</a>, 1992</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:322px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Breakup_of_Yugoslavia.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Breakup_of_Yugoslavia.gif/320px-Breakup_of_Yugoslavia.gif" width="320" height="257" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Breakup_of_Yugoslavia.gif/480px-Breakup_of_Yugoslavia.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Breakup_of_Yugoslavia.gif/640px-Breakup_of_Yugoslavia.gif 2x" data-file-width="1545" data-file-height="1242" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Breakup_of_Yugoslavia.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Dissolution process of <a href="/wiki/SFRJ" class="mw-redirect" title="SFRJ">SFRJ</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On 18 November 1990, multi-party parliamentary elections were held throughout Bosnia and Herzegovina. A second round followed on 25 November, resulting in a <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly" title="National Assembly">national assembly</a> where communist power was replaced by a <a href="/wiki/Coalition" title="Coalition">coalition</a> of three ethnically-based parties.<sup id="cite_ref-phron1_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-phron1-69">[69]</a></sup> Following <a href="/wiki/Slovenia" title="Slovenia">Slovenia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">Croatia</a>'s declarations of independence from Yugoslavia, a significant split developed among the residents of Bosnia and Herzegovina on the issue of whether to remain within Yugoslavia (overwhelmingly favored by Serbs) or seek independence (overwhelmingly favored by Bosniaks and Croats).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The Serb members of parliament, consisting mainly of the <a href="/wiki/Serbian_Democratic_Party_(Bosnia_and_Herzegovina)" class="mw-redirect" title="Serbian Democratic Party (Bosnia and Herzegovina)">Serb Democratic Party</a> members, abandoned the central parliament in Sarajevo, and formed the <a href="/wiki/National_Assembly_of_the_Republika_Srpska" class="mw-redirect" title="National Assembly of the Republika Srpska">Assembly of the Serb People of Bosnia and Herzegovina</a> on 24 October 1991, which marked the end of the tri-ethnic coalition that governed after the elections in 1990. This Assembly established the Serbian Republic of Bosnia and Herzegovina on 9 January 1992, which was renamed <a href="/wiki/Republika_Srpska" title="Republika Srpska">Republika Srpska</a> in August 1992.
